The Tales that Bind: Toward a Narrative Model of Rural Helping

Author: Randall, William1; Clews, Rosemary1 Dept Gerontology, Saint Thomas U, Fredericton, New Brunswick randall@stthomasu.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61497992?accountid=14667

Abstract: In this paper, we explore the potential of a narrative model to assist social workers & other helpers to practice in rural communities. We outline "meta-narratives" about rural communities & argue that they do not convey the richness & diversity of rural life. As an alternative, we suggest that a model containing four "angles" -- the poetical, the hermeneutic, the therapeutic, & the political -- can enhance helpers' awareness of unique qualities of the rural communities where they practice. 63 References. Adapted from the source document.

Pre-Service Education and Qualification -- The Impact on Recruitment and Retention in Rural Child Protection

Author: Gibbs, Judith11 LaTrobe U, Albury Wodonga, Victoria, Australia j.gibbs@aw.latrobe.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61486304?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper discusses the findings from a study that examined how rural child protection workers viewed the relevance of their pre-service training & preparation for the job, & how this entered their decision making about first, coming into the program, & second, remaining in the job. Typically, workers were found to have drifted into child protection because it appeared to be a good stepping-stone to other career opportunities in the field, & many described themselves as poorly prepared for the role. Workers who had completed a field education placement in child protection or who had prior experience appeared to have greater resilience. The paper includes a discussion about the implications for the academic institutions responsible for education & training of workers & the need for far greater collaboration between the agency workplace & academic institutions. 34 References. Adapted from the source document.

Social Work Intervention in the Colonias: Opportunities for Student Learning

Author: Hatchett, Bonnie F1; Garcia, Celia Cruz1 Social Work Program, U Texas, El Paso hatchett@utep.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61446782?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper highlights experiences of social work interns placed in The Kellogg Community Partnerships program health clinics in colonias along the TX-Mexico border. Sociocultural barriers as well as considerations for successful intervention in a rural area are presented. The term "colonias" is used to describe physically & legally isolated rural & unincorporated subdivisions of US cities located along the TX-Mexico international boundary that are characterized by poverty, substandard housing, & inadequate access to clean water & limited access to health care. The mission of The Kellogg Community Partnerships program is to enhance the quality of life of the participating underserved communities & to improve the quality of health profession education through inter-institutional/community collaboration on health services & education. Interns found that cultural sensitivity, knowledge of resources in the geographical area, & language proficiency are necessary for successful intervention. 10 References. Adapted from the source document.

Using Constructs for Self-Appraisal: A Tool for Social Work Practitioners

Author: Travers, Denis11 School Social Work & Welfare Studies, Central Queensland U, Mackay, Australia d.travers@cqu.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61536580?accountid=14667

Abstract: Social workers practice in a welfare state ravaged by economic rationalism. The majority of them have inadequate supervision. These marginalizing influences are frequently exacerbated for rural practitioners, who can become professionally as well as geographically isolated. The effects are evident in practitioners feeling abandoned, dissatisfied, & burn out. This paper describes the trial of a method aimed at helping social work practitioners cope with the effects of marginalization by reflecting on their practice in a structured way. The results of the pilot study suggest that, with some further technical development, this method has the potential to assist practitioners to develop an idealized image of competent social work practice, & to be consequently inspired to strive for excellence in their own practice. 13 Tables, 34 References. Adapted from the source document.

Surviving Globalization: Empowering Rural and Remote Communities in Canada's Provincial Norths

Author: Delaney, Roger1; Brownlee, Keith; Sellick, Margaret1 Dept Social Work, Lakehead U, Thunder Bay, Ontario rdelaney@gale.lakeheadu.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61537863?accountid=14667

Abstract: Globalization is emerging as the dominant narrative informing modern political economy & social constructions of economic necessity. National autonomy & local culture are being transformed to accommodate to these forces. It is a pattern that renders Canada's rural or remote regions, such as Canada's Provincial Norths, particularly vulnerable to the negative consequences of such transformations. This is especially so given the history of exploitation, oppression & political isolation in these regions. If rural & remote communities in the Provincial Norths are going to survive the effects of globalization, two strategies are required. First, the strengthening of communities through the reconstruction of narratives about the community's strengths & abilities &, second, community economic development, which includes social development practices. Empowering communities to engage both local & global issues will require the social worker to respond with context-sensitive & context-aware practices that are respectful & inclusive of local narratives, wisdom, traditions, & knowledge. 29 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Surviving+Globalization%3A+Empowering+Rural+and+Remote+Communities+in+Canada%27s+Provincial+Norths&amp;rft.au=Delaney%2C+Roger%3BBrownlee%2C+Keith%3BSellick%2C+Margaret&amp;rft.aulast=Delaney&amp;rft.aufirst=Roger&amp;rft.date=2001-12-01&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.spage=4&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Notions of Rurality

Author: Zapf, Michael Kim11 BSW Access Division, Faculty Social Work, U Calgary, Alberta kzapf@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61537911?accountid=14667

Abstract: Any serious discussion of rural social work in the global era must be grounded in some common understanding of both globalization and rurality. The recent literature offers some convergence on the concept of globalization, but there remains considerable confusion over what constitutes rurality. This article examines the notion of rurality as it has been presented in the knowledge base for rural social work. Rural myths & stereotypes are identified. Attempts to define rural areas using the variable of population are explored, followed by consideration of rural indices & continua. A working definition of rurality is proposed incorporating variables of population, isolation, economic activity, & social systems. Diversity within & among rural regions is examined, & the emerging distinction between rural & remote regions is addressed. The article concludes with comments regarding the connections between globalization & rurality. 104 References. Adapted from the source document.

Globalisation and Rural Communities

Author: Cheers, Brian11 Centre Rural & Remote Area Studies, U South Australia, Whyalla Norrie e-mail:brian.cheers@unisa.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61472547?accountid=14667

Abstract: Using Australia as a case study, this paper reviews the impacts of transnational & national changes in market processes, politics, ecology, technology, & demography on rural & Indigenous people & their communities. It is argued that these impacts have exposed the long-standing fundamental inadequacies of mainstream Western approaches to social welfare in responding to the needs, contexts, & ways of living of rural & Indigenous people. In brief, these approaches have been a major reason why rural people around the world have been disadvantaged relative to those living in urban places. Alternative ideas are presented that will be more successful in meeting the needs of rural & Indigenous people. Prominent among these is the concept of "rural social care." In essence, social care comprises all those arrangements & processes -- both formal structures & ongoing informal interaction -- through which people meet each other's social, emotional, & material needs. We professionals are not the center of the social caring universe. 1 Table, 45 References. Adapted from the source document.

Globalisation, Fragmentation and the Analysis of Difference in Rural Social Work

Author: Pugh, Richard11 School Social Relations, Keele U, Staffordshire, UK pughrg@aol.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61514005?accountid=14667

Abstract: The existence of discrimination toward ethnic minorities in rural areas is being increasingly acknowledged, & this has raised awareness of previously "invisible" injustices. While there is a continuing need to combat discrimination & make visible such inequities, arguably, the theoretical frameworks within which difference & discrimination in rural areas are analyzed are underdeveloped. Understandably, given the changes being wrought upon many rural communities, globalization appears a promising perspective from which to understand these phenomena. The main aim of this paper is to consider what impact the phenomenon termed fragmentation might have upon rural communities & to indicate how it might inform our analyses of the position of minorities in rural areas. It suggests that the impact of globalization upon social cohesion is overstated, but nonetheless, acknowledges that globalization usefully focuses attention upon difference in rural communities & especially upon how changing perceptions of social stability & social identity may impact upon minorities. The paper concludes with a review of the import of these notions for the understandings of difference that inform rural practice. 37 References. Adapted from the source document.

Hepatitis C: Social Justice Concerns and Global Health Needs

Author: Taylor, Laura E11 School Social Work, U Windsor, Ontario taylorla@uwindsor.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61484220?accountid=14667

Abstract: Hepatitis C, which has reached epidemic proportions throughout the world, is enmeshed in issues related to social justice. The issues facing those infected & affected residing in rural areas are of particular concern to social work practitioners who will find themselves without user-friendly resource materials, & without supportive counseling groups because of the fear of community stigma if identified with Hepatitis C. Furthermore, public awareness of Hepatitis C is limited as this new disease is just beginning to receive widespread publicity in many parts of the world. Both urban & rural social work practitioners have acknowledged that they lack professional knowledge about Hepatitis C & the specialized counseling needs for individuals, families, & communities because Hepatitis C is one of the "newest" reportable diseases & is not included in social work curricula. In developed countries, much of the focus of concern related to Hepatitis C tends to be directed toward large, urban centers. However, particularly given the "silent" nature & long dormancy period of this illness, the threat of Hepatitis C as a health issue in rural & remote communities must not be underestimated. 32 References. Adapted from the source document.

Palliative Care in Rural Canada

Author: MacLean, Michael J1; Kelley, Mary Lou1 Faculty Social Work, U Regina, Saskatchewan michael.maclean@uregina.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61446789?accountid=14667

Abstract: Palliative care is practiced in rural Canada, but the components of this practice have not been documented. The purpose of this paper is to present information on the components of a rural palliative care model based on the experiences of social work & health care professionals providing palliative care in rural Canada. Qualitative data were analyzed from 64 formal caregivers in focus groups in six regions of Canada: the Atlantic Region (Halifax, Charlottetown, Baddeck), Ontario (Thunder Bay, Dryden), Manitoba (Beausejour), British Columbia (Saltspring Island), & the Yukon Territories (Whitehorse). The results of these focus groups outline significant components of the participants' experiences of providing rural palliative care that leads to a beginning model of palliative care in rural Canada. 25 References. Adapted from the source document.

Aging People in Aging Places: Addressing the Needs of Older Adults in Rural Saskatchewan

Author: MacKenzie, Patricia11 School Social Work, U Victoria, British Columbia patmack@uvic.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61430482?accountid=14667

Abstract: While many elderly Canadians live in an urban or suburban environment, about 17% of those aged 65+ live in rural communities. Communities that provide the social setting for individual aging have difficulty in developing or retaining adequate resources to meet their unique needs for service, particularly given the negative effect of globalization on rural economic activity. The threat to rural sustainability is of concern to all rural residents but is of critical importance & concern to the aging residents of rural provinces such as Saskatchewan. Services that specifically address the needs of older rural residents have been neglected in many parts of the country. Based on research conducted in Saskatchewan, this article identifies the challenges of growing older in an aging, rural community & makes specific recommendations for improving services to rural elders. Social work practitioners need to become more active in recognizing these needs & pressing for program & service development. There is significant scope for social work practitioners to address the decline of rural infrastructures & to develop creative social programs that will respond to the needs of emerging populations such as elderly people who are "aging in place.". 46 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Aging+People+in+Aging+Places%3A+Addressing+the+Needs+of+Older+Adults+in+Rural+Saskatchewan&amp;rft.au=MacKenzie%2C+Patricia&amp;rft.aulast=MacKenzie&amp;rft.aufirst=Patricia&amp;rft.date=2001-12-01&amp;rft.volume=6&amp;rft.issue=3&amp;rft.spage=74&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Assessing Needs for Rural Child Welfare Work in the United States When Mothers Have Mental Illness

Author: Raske, Martha11 Social Work Dept, U Southern Indiana, Evansville mraske@usi.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61498022?accountid=14667

Abstract: Using focus groups with rural workers in the US, this study examined the knowledge, skills, & values needed for work when mothers have mental illness. Findings showed that, in rural areas, workers needed knowledge about maternal mental illness & its impact on parental functioning & child development. Specialized skills were needed to communicate with mothers about their mental health problems & the impact of these problems on children. The study raised questions about the prevalence of maternal mental illness in child welfare caseloads & rural resources needed to cope with these high-risk families. Implications for rural practice are discussed. 4 Tables, 40 References. Adapted from the source document.

Using Technology in Rural Practice -- Local Area Coordination in Rural Australia

Author: Chenoweth, Lesley1; Stehlik, Daniela1 School Social Work & Social Policy, U Queensland, Brisbane, Australia l.chenoweth@social.uq.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61486335?accountid=14667

Abstract: The use of information technology in human service practice in rural & remote areas has been increasingly promoted in Australia over the past 5 years. Federally funded initiatives such as Networking the Nation have sought to provide cheaper & efficient Internet & e-mail access for people living in rural & remote areas of the continent. The use of such technologies in the delivery of human services has been somewhat ambivalent -- both eagerly embraced & viewed with skepticism (Cheers, 1996; Martinez-Brawley, 2000). One Queensland initiative that has embraced the possibilities of technologies in providing services to people with disabilities & their families in remote areas is Local Area Coordination (LAC). Individual Local Area Coordinators, based in 1 remote & 5 rural centers, use a range of information technologies in their daily work. This includes providing support & advocacy to people with disabilities & their families as well as community work to increase community inclusion. This paper draws upon the findings of a research study that evaluated the LAC pilot project. The study spent time with practitioners & discussed with them their experiences in the use of such technology for 18 months. This paper examines the dilemmas raised by the increased use of information technologies -- dilemmas such as intrusiveness & confidentiality, reliability, equity of access, & training. The implications for rural & remote human services are discussed. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

Impacts of Rural Culture on Domestic Violence

Author: Wendt, Sarah1; Cheers, Brian1 Centre Rural & Remote Area Studies, U South Australia, Whyalla Norrie swendt@arcom.com.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61514019?accountid=14667

Abstract: Although it has frequently been suggested that aspects of rural culture present barriers to women escaping domestic violence, research has not yet focused on how rural culture impacts on their experiences. This paper reports a study that explored how 14 women experiencing domestic violence perceived local cultural beliefs & values, the extent to which they had internalized these, & how they believed rural culture impacted on them in their situations. Components of their local rural cultures that they identified as impacting on their experiences of domestic violence included belief in the sanctity & permanence of marriage, the importance & privacy of the nuclear family, Christian doctrine, & preservation of intergenerational property transfer. Each woman's story showed that while rural culture gave them strength to endure the violence it also created internal conflicts between wanting to escape & the cultural beliefs & values that they had internalized. They were also afraid of community reactions should they leave. Consequently, they did not disclose their violent situations & had persevered in them far longer than they thought they would have in a different cultural context. 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Impacts+of+Rural+Culture+on+Domestic+Violence&amp;rft.au=Wendt%2C+Sarah%3BCheers%2C+Brian&amp;rft.aulast=Wendt&amp;rft.aufirst=Sarah&amp;rft.date=2002-06-01&amp;rft.volume=7&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.spage=22&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Managing Confidentiality in Rural Welfare Practice in Australia

Author: Green, Rosemary1; Mason, Robyn1 School Behavioural/Social Sciences & Humanities, U Ballarat, Mount Helen, Victoria, Australia r.green@ballarat.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61485859?accountid=14667

Abstract: In rural areas, managing confidentiality can be complex. There are three issues to be addressed for professional practice in rural areas: guarding privileged knowledge, use of client-related knowledge gained informally, & rural service delivery considerations that protect client privacy & confidentiality. In 2000, a research project, sponsored by the U of Ballarat, explored these issues with rural social work & welfare practitioners in the Grampians Region of Victoria. Using a range of methods, including mailed questionnaires (23 respondents), forums (attendance approximately 40), & six individual, in-depth interviews, questions were asked relating to rural practice, & particularly professional & personal boundaries. Significant concern was expressed about managing dual & multiple relationships where information is received in a variety of ways. Other concerns highlighted the dilemmas confronting rural social welfare workers about community expectations & duty to warn, & protecting clients from gossip & innuendo. 22 References. Adapted from the source document.

I Am Not as Cool as I Thought I Was: The Challenges of Conducting Research with Young People in Rural Areas

Author: Geldens, Paula M11 Instit Land & Food Resources, U Melbourne, Burnley, Richmond, Victoria, Australia p.geldens@pgrad.unimelb.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61485880?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper explores a journey of both professional & self-discovery, similar, I am sure, to that undertaken by all who, in the course of their lives or careers, find themselves investing in the lives of the young. This account is based on my experiences as I negotiated with several groups of young people from family farms their participation in a project about their decisions regarding their futures. More specifically, their decision about whether to remain on the family farm, continuing a legacy or family farming tradition, or, alternately, their decision to pursue a different future for themselves. As a young researcher, conducting research with young people naturally presents certain challenges. The potential implications of self-presentation, insider/outsider status, & the compounding influence of obtaining written parental consent upon participation rates in research involving school-aged participants are explored. 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

Author: Bodor, Ralph1; Zapf, Michael Kim1 Faculty Social Work, U Calgary, Alberta rcbodor@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61496927?accountid=14667

Abstract: During the 1980s, issues of relevant social work education for isolated rural regions were highlighted in the literature from Australia & Canada. Building on the reported experiences of outreach social work education programs since that time, this paper examines the new Learning Circle model as developed & delivered by the Faculty of Social Work at the U of Calgary. Developed in consultation with stakeholder groups in the community & the profession, the Learning Circle model features many curriculum & delivery innovations to ensure cultural & geographic relevance for rural, remote, & Aboriginal communities across the province of Alberta. 1 Figure, 22 References. Adolescent Dating Violence in the Rural Context

Author: Olimb, Darlene1; Brownlee, Keith; Tranter, David1 c/o Brownlee -- School Social Work, Lakehead U, Thunder Bay, Ontario

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61498077?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper examines adolescent dating violence in rural communities. It is argued that dating violence, especially dating violence in rural areas, is a topic that has received insufficient attention in the social work literature. It is proposed in this paper that characteristics of rural communities, eg, rural attitudes & values, rural social relations, isolation, & the lack of services & recreation are all contextual factors that play a role in dating violence. The influence of these contextual variables upon dating violence is discussed, & it is suggested that interventions that are designed to address dating violence in rural areas need to consider such contextual issues. 57 References. Adapted from the source document.

Rural Domestic Violence: Moving towards Feminist Poststructural Understandings

Author: Wendt, Sarah1; Taylor, Judy; Kennedy, Marie1 Centre Rural & Regional Development, U South Australia, Whyalla Norrie swendt@arcom.com.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61496948?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper provides a critique of the Australian research into rural domestic violence. Research to date has focused on the factors that keep rural women trapped in violent relationships. While this research has been useful in developing policy to address rural domestic violence, it has not yet provided information about women's understandings of their rural contexts. Research into domestic violence is moving toward acknowledging & recognizing the complexities & differences between people's experiences. It is time to explore the differences between various rural regions, & to move away from the assumption that there is one rural culture. A move toward feminist poststructural perspectives has strengths in that it enables a focus on the meanings of rural cultures from the perspectives of women who experience, & men who perpetrate, domestic violence. If these meanings become apparent, it may enable local solutions to be implemented & contribute knowledge & new ideas. 56 References. Adapted from the source document.

Understanding Community Participation in Rural Health Service Development

Author: Taylor, Judy11 Spencer Gulf Rural Health School, U South Australia, Whyalla Norrie judy.taylor@unisa.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61430845?accountid=14667

Abstract: The national & international literature discussing the role of community participation in health is extensive. This paper identifies, from this literature, three conceptual approaches relevant to understanding community participation in health services development -- the 'contributions' approach, the 'instrumental' approach, & the 'developmental' approach. In exploring these approaches, an example of community participation in organizing a model for general practice in a rural community in South Australia is discussed. This community demonstrated a long-standing tradition of support for the district hospital extending to the general practice when it became difficult to recruit a general practitioner. The community understood community participation as a developmental process. However, it is argued that the example, from a public policy perspective, demonstrates a contributions approach to community participation with the community primarily perceived as a 'contributor' to development rather than as the developer. The implications of this in developing community participation processes in health service development, particularly in relation to rural general practice, are discussed. 39 References. Adapted from the source document.

Social Work and the Prevention of Sexual Violence in Rural Communities: The Ties That Bind

Author: Rawsthorne, Margot

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61549629?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article highlights the challenges facing social workers in the prevention of sexual violence in rural communities in Australia. It draws on doctoral research undertaken in two rural communities in the State of New South Wales. Through in-depth interviews with young people ranging from 13 to 19 years of age the study highlighted the complex negotiations facing young people in rural settings as they become sexual. The study also highlighted the challenges facing social work & other practitioners working in the area of prevention of sexual violence in rural settings. It argues that the ties formed by social workers in rural settings will add to these challenges & proposes an alternative conceptual framework to the orthodox notions of community development. 1 Table, 31 References. Social Impact of NGOs in Alleviating Rural Poverty in Bangladesh

Author: Ullah, AKM Ahsan1; Routray, Jayant K1 Dept Sociology, National U Singapore

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61503613?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper analyzes the contribution of NGOs to alleviating rural poverty in Bangladesh using different indicators of social well-being of the beneficiary households, which include housing conditions, violence, empowerment, hygiene practice such as access to safe water & sanitary latrine use, morbidity patterns, & health-seeking behavior. The data-collection exercise was aimed at gathering information on the impacts of social interventions of NGOs by investigating pre- & post-NGO situations of the NGO beneficiary households. It was revealed that housing conditions had improved significantly between the pre- & post-NGO periods. A significantly higher percentage of beneficiary households had access to safe water & sanitary latrines in the post- than the pre-NGO situation. Nearly two-thirds of the households suffered from some forms of morbidity. A significantly higher percentage of households visited MBBS & kabiraj with various complaints. A substantial number of rural people, despite their involvement in NGOS, visit traditional doctors when they fall sick. It was found that NGO interventions have played an important role in empowering women in rural Bangladesh. Significant improvement was visible for beneficiary households on most indicators of social well-being. 12 Tables, 2 Figures, 35 References. Adapted from the source document.

Integrated Human Services in Rural Areas: A Social Work Approach

Author: Linzmayer, Cara

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61503637?accountid=14667

Abstract: There has been a recent shift in human service delivery in Saskatchewan towards collaborative & integrative approaches. With funding cutbacks characterizing today's human services, provincial governments are pressuring organizations into collaborative ventures. To enter into integration with resistance or defensiveness is to miss out on a valuable opportunity. Integration is intended to improve service delivery & make it more cost effective. This approach has significant implications for rural Canada. Creating a rural model of integrated service delivery can be an important step towards rural development. There is a plethora of research that indicates there is a marked difference in the provision of human services in rural & urban areas. This paper will weave the experiences of nine integrative projects throughout rural Saskatchewan into a workable model that will then be evaluated by regional planners & potential human service users. Social work & community implications for an integrated human services model for rural communities will be discussed. 58 References. Adapted from the source document.

Extent of Service Coordination in Rural South Australia

Author: Munn, Peter1; Cheers, Brian; Petkov, John1 Centre Rural & Regional Development, U South Australia, Whyalla Norrie peter.munn@unisa.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61478615?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article discusses the results of a study of the extent of service coordination in nine rural & remote towns in South Australia. The study adopted questionnaire procedures to seek information on the nature of the elements affecting coordination & the type of strategies that exist for human service professionals working in these communities; 327 people completed the questionnaire. The study revealed that information sharing & networking were the key coordination factors & the most prevalent coordination strategy was what Mulford & Rogers (1982) refer to as a 'mutual adjustment' strategy. 9 Tables, 2 Figures, 20 References. Adapted from the source document.

Only in Exceptional Circumstances!: Education in Australia for Rural Social Work and Welfare Practice

Author: Green, Rosemary11 School Behavioural/Social Sciences/Humanities, U Ballarat, Victoria, Australia r.green@ballarat.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61496531?accountid=14667

Abstract: Rural welfare practice requires a mature, flexible, & community-embedded approach, with highly developed skills in a range of practice approaches. The management of ethical dilemmas & role blurring in highly interactive communities is a feature of rural practice. For those working in these areas, such issues are critical. Rural practice requires an appreciation of context at a sophisticated level, & an understanding of how the context impacts not only on the clients & community, but also on the welfare or social worker & their practice. This paper provides an overview of the importance placed on rural practice by educational institutions teaching welfare & social work in Australia by examining published Web-based information on courses. The results were compared to findings from Sturmey's (1992) study where aspects of rural & remote practice within social work & welfare courses were identified by means of self-report through questionnaires. The findings indicate that little has changed in a decade of social work & welfare education, & that the public emphasis on rural practice issues by institutions is almost nonexistent. 3 Tables, 23 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Only+in+Exceptional+Circumstances%21%3A+Education+in+Australia+for+Rural+Social+Work+and+Welfare+Practice&amp;rft.au=Green%2C+Rosemary&amp;rft.aulast=Green&amp;rft.aufirst=Rosemary&amp;rft.date=2003-06-01&amp;rft.volume=8&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.spage=50&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Youth Participation in a Rural Community Centre: The Case of Southwest Victoria

Author: O'Toole, Kevin11 School Social & International Studies, Deakin U, Warnambool, Victoria, Australia otoole@deakin.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61451114?accountid=14667

Abstract: The place of 'youth centers' in youth policy has become quite problematic since the development of 'drop-in' centers in the 1970s. However there is still an expressed need by many youth to have access to nonthreatening environments & processes that do not alienate them. A range of youth consultations over the past decade has seen the same theme emerge in southwest Victoria. This article analyzes the development of a concept for a community youth complex in southwest Victoria. It begins with a discussion of youth, social capital, & participation. The article then surveys the literature on rural youth in a changing social-economic environment. After a discussion of the views of youth in southwest Victoria derived from the range of consultations over the past few years, the article then looks at the transition from a 'youth center' to a 'community youth complex.' The last section looks at the underlying ideas & mechanisms of the proposed 'community youth complex'. The article concludes by summarizing the underlying approach to active citizenship used by Brophy Family & Youth Services for the complex. 1 Table, 1 Figure, 42 References. Adapted from the source document.

Rural Health Care Intervention: Medical Social Work Practice with Adults with Physical and Chronic Disabilities

Author: Levy, Patricia11 Dept Sociology & Social Work, Fort Hays State U, KS plevy@fhsu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61500704?accountid=14667

Abstract: Medical social workers in rural health care settings must have a special appreciation of the cultural values & strengths of the people & the communities they serve. Professional awareness of the manner in which socioeconomic, political, & cultural beliefs have impacted on the adjustment & adaptation of adult populations with chronic illness & disability are critical to the employment of methods of assessment, planning, & professional intervention. In addition, rapidly changing health care environments in America have expanded the social work roles within the restructuring of social service offerings within medical settings. Information has been incorporated from three interviews demonstrating the cyclical effects of psychosocial & health factors on micro, mezzo, & macro levels of medical social work intervention. 33 References. Adapted from the source document.

Dams and Displacement: Who Fills the Bellies of Oustees

Author: Barik, Bishnu C11 School Social Sciences, Swami Ramanand Teerth Marathwada U, Vishnupuri, Nanded, India bishnu_barik@rediffmail.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61497011?accountid=14667

Abstract: Dams are constructed with legitimization in the goals of providing irrigation to agriculture, consumption in the domestic sector, & producing power. Basically, dams are conceived as agents of human advance. But, over the years, the emerged reality suggests that dams have also caused huge sufferings, in the life of marginalized groups like 'scheduled tribes,' 'scheduled castes,' & 'other backward classes,' who became prey to the involuntary dislocations. Based on factual information, the author tries to highlight the fact that the interest of these marginalized sections have been totally neglected & jeopardized. This has happened in almost all the major development projects. That is why the author raises a rational question: who fills the bellies of oustees? 3 Tables, 16 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Dams+and+Displacement%3A+Who+Fills+the+Bellies+of+Oustees&amp;rft.au=Barik%2C+Bishnu+C&amp;rft.aulast=Barik&amp;rft.aufirst=Bishnu&amp;rft.date=2003-12-01&amp;rft.volume=8&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=28&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

A Little More Conversation: Why Farm Inheritance and Family Provision Legislation Needs Discussion

Author: Charleston, Andrew

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61500682?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article describes the issues that surround Farm Inheritance & Family Provision Legislation & why in the current rural & economic climate the discussion of such issues is important. The author focuses on the typical way in which farms are transferred, the general application of succession law to the family farm, & the role & recognition of women in farming life. There is also a critical analysis of the legal recourses available for neglected family members & an examination of the potential value of detailed succession plans. 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

The Place of Care -- Rural Human Services on the Fringe

Author: Cheers, Brian11 Centre Rural & Regional Development, U South Australia, Whyalla brian.cheers@unisa.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61433776?accountid=14667

Abstract: This keynote speech from a conference in May 2003 (in Halifax) on the provision of human services in rural areas summarizes the experiences of "front-end" social service providers, or those who work directly with rural populations. Providing the most appropriate services is a balancing act between the needs of rural populations & the mandates of bureaucracies. That balancing act is further complicated by the ever-changing needs of rural populations in a world that is moving past modernity into still relatively unknown territory. An emphasis on developmental, participatory, & community-based research, & an attention to the life narratives created by members of the communities will help human services workers meet the specific, fluid needs of rural communities, communities that have been badly served by the forces & narratives of modernity. 2 Figures, 51 References. D. Knaff

Hardly Worth Mentioning Groups and the Informal Community

Author: Banks, Ken11 School Social Work, U Buffalo, State U New York banks@niagara.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61526800?accountid=14667

Abstract: Is the local capacity to maintain community compromised when old towns are absorbed into suburban sprawl? In Canada's Province of Ontario there is a golden horseshoe' of development within a perimeter of approximately one hundred kilometers of the capital city of Toronto. Many old towns & villages that were of exemplary vitality one hundred years ago are now reduced to postal addresses, mere names of sections in regional cities. This process has gone on from time immemorial, but community workers may be interested in themes that contributed to the old capacities & whether community life can be sustained. The Company of Neighbours action research project examined one of the vital old towns in the golden horseshoe, shortly after it had been amalgamated into a new regional municipality. This paper discusses action research methods used to identify vestiges of community & an apparent misapprehension that local people had of their loss of a voice in the sustenance of community life. 34 References. Adapted from the source document.

Social Work Opportunities in Rural Development: Lithuania 1991-2004

Author: Pozzuto, Richard1; Juska, Aruas; Angell, G Brent; Johnstone, Peter1 School Social Work, Coll Human Economy, East Carolina U, Greenville, NC pozzutor@mail.ecu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61562717?accountid=14667

Abstract: Post-Soviet Baltic & Eastern European countries offer rural social work numerous opportunities for rural development initiatives. Some of the typical issues & opportunities in those regions are illustrated by Lithuania during the period 1991 to 2004. Lithuania's transformation to a post-Soviet, free market economy reduced social services, accentuated social stratification, & increased social anomie. Those ills are in sharp contrast to the positive outcomes of "bottom-up" rural community development movements found in the small Lithuanian village of Balninkai. This article uses Berger & Luckman's (1966) constructivist framework to understand Lithuania's current situation & to identify potential approaches for rural development by social workers based on the example of Balninkai. Implications from the discussion include a need for detailed comparative studies of successful & unsuccessful community development attempts in various post-Soviet Baltic & Eastern European settings in order to improve the theoretical & empirical foundation for rural development initiatives there & elsewhere. 32 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Social+Work+Opportunities+in+Rural+Development%3A+Lithuania+1991-2004&amp;rft.au=Pozzuto%2C+Richard%3BJuska%2C+Aruas%3BAngell%2C+G+Brent%3BJohnstone%2C+Peter&amp;rft.aulast=Pozzuto&amp;rft.aufirst=Richard&amp;rft.date=2004-12-01&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=42&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

40 Degrees above or 40 Degrees below Zero: Rural Social Work and Context in Australia and Canada

Author: Bodor, Ralph1; Green, Rosemary; Lonne, Bob; Zapf, Michael Kim1 Faculty Social Work, U Calgary, Alberta rcbodor@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61518265?accountid=14667

Abstract: The past decade has witnessed significant interest in delivery of human services in rural & remote regions. However, there has been little exploration in the literature of the contextual similarities or differences in human services as delivered in different countries, resulting in uncertainty about the extent to which local circumstances are common around the globe or unique to that context. Australia & Canada, for example, appear to share many characteristics while, at the same time, there are apparent key differences between the two countries when it comes to such things as climate, population density & spread, community size & proximity to the direct influence of the United States. 53 References. Adapted from the source document.

Collaborative Methodologies: Evaluating an Inuit Social Work Education Initiative

Author: Bella, Leslie1; Lyall, Brian1 School Social Work, Memorial U, St John's, Newfoundland lbella@mu.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61562238?accountid=14667

Abstract: Collaborative processes were used to evaluate a social work education initiative involving the Labrador Inuit Association (L.I.A.) & the School of Social Work at Memorial University of Newfoundland (M.U.N.). Together the two organisations developed & successfully delivered a social work diploma to a cohort of ten Inuit students in coastal Labrador. To understand this success we undertook an evaluation, using the same collaborative processes used throughout the programme. Programme graduates surveyed all those associated with the programme (including University administrators, instructors, students, employers, & Labrador Inuit leadership). In a subsequent workshop, representatives of the participating organisations & of the programme graduates jointly constructed a programme narrative, & analysed the survey results. The result was an evaluation report that influenced other professional education initiatives for Aboriginal groups in the region. 14 References. Adapted from the source document.

Early Intervention Parenting Assistance for Rural and Remote Families in South Australia

Author: Burgess, Christine1; Cheers, Brian; Fisher, Donna1 Gowrie Training Centre, Thebarton, Australia christineb@gowrie-adelaide.com.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61512118?accountid=14667

Abstract: Parent education programs are an effective means of preventing or minimising misbehaviour in children. Their purpose is to teach parents positive strategies for interacting with & disciplining their children & to create opportunities for learning & growth. When these interventions are utilised in the early stages of a child's life, the chance of developing serious behavioural problems is reduced. The Whyalla Counselling Service Incorporated, in partnership with the Centre for Rural & Regional Development at the University of South Australia, has been delivering the Whyalla & Upper Eyre Peninsula Parenting Project' (WUEPPP) to rural & remote families in South Australia since 2001. The aim of this project is to deliver parent education services to rural & remote towns located on the Eyre Peninsula in western South Australia. The project specifically consists of providing the Positive Parenting Program, otherwise known as 'Triple P', to families within this area. Participatory action research has been utilised to assist with developing the program to better service Indigenous & other communities within this region. This project has focused on creating & maintaining partnerships with local key stakeholders & community members in rural & remote locations to ensure the provision of an effective outreach service. 3 Figures, 26 References. Adapted from the source document.

Promoting Youth Citizenship in Rural Quebec: An Evaluation

Author: Chiasson, Guy1; Savard, Sebastien1 Social Work & Social Sciences Dept, U Quebec Outaouais, Gatineau guy.chiasson@uqo.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61512079?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper presents the results of an evaluation study conducted on the Mobilisation jeunesse en milieu rural project. This project was involved with youth from 18 rural communities in Abitibi-Temiscamingue, one of Quebec's (Canada) most peripheral & resource-driven regions. More specifically, our evaluation assessed, through a scrutiny of both its process & end results, Mobilisation jeunesse's ability to promote a fuller citizenship for youth in their communities. We show that the program only partially reaches its goals in terms of youth integration & that these mitigated results can be in large part traced back to some difficulties in the process. The results of our study bring forth the necessity to revisit youth citizenship in order to develop conceptual as well as practical tools that are more adapted to the reality of adolescents in the rural periphery. 28 References. Adapted from the source document.

Reaching Out to Homeless Rural Youth: The Whyalla and Upper Eyre Peninsula Reconnect Project

Author: Farrin, Jane1; Cheers, Brian; Jones, Joanne; Venning, Emily1 School Psychology, U South Australia, Adelaide jane.farrin@unisa.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61453854?accountid=14667

Abstract: The Whyalla & Upper Eyre Peninsula Reconnect Service provides early intervention & prevention services for youth homelessness in rural & remote South Australia by re-engaging homeless youth & those at risk of homelessness with mainstream institutions in society. The project services both Indigenous & non-Indigenous communities. It is a joint project of the University of South Australia, Whyalla Youth Services & Plaza Youth Centre. By integrating participatory action research methods with service provision, intervention strategies are being devised that are tailored to each young person, their family, & their community. In this paper we present the literature relating to rural youth homelessness, trace the development of the service, discuss the research & evaluation strategies employed, present the results of this research, & identify how these have shaped the service. 1 Figure, 12 References. Adapted from the source document.

Rural Cultures and Domestic Violence: Stories from Human Service Workers Living in South Australian Wine Country

Author: Wendt, Sarah1; Cheers, Brian1 Keele U, Staffordshire, UK s.c.wendt@appsoc.keele.ac.uk

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61497182?accountid=14667

Abstract: Images of rural communities such as 'true blue Aussies', tough, rugged & resilient lifestyles, nobility of farmers, harmonious people, & beautiful scenery are common in Australia. Rural people are perceived as being more traditional & conservative than city people. However, despite such frequent impressions in the literature, there is surprisingly little research on how various aspects of rural cultures impact on women's experiences, & men's perpetration, of domestic violence. The purpose of this paper is to share results of a study in rural South Australia that explored this issue using feminist, post-structural frameworks. The methodology involved detailed analysis of local rural cultural narratives & their effects on domestic violence relationships in the Barossa Valley region. Stories told by human service workers living & working in rural communities are presented to identify dominant cultural discourses that impact on domestic violence. Components of local cultures that were frequently spoken about include the importance & influence of religious values & beliefs, the importance of family & inter-generational property transfer, & valuing German heritage & self-reliance. By exploring cultural discourses in rural communities, questions concerning whether rural cultures actually contribute to the production & maintenance of domestic violence are starting to be answered. 2 Figures, 25 References. Adapted from the source document.

Resistance and Change: Building a Framework for Helping Abused Rural Women

Author: Hornosty, Jennie1; Doherty, Deborah1 U New Brunswick, Fredericton hornosty@unb.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/60535958?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper presents the findings of research conducted in New Brunswick with rural service providers who work with abused women. It points to the uniqueness of rural communities & suggests that service providers have much to teach us about working with abused women in a rural setting. Building a framework for action requires knowledge, not only of the strengths & capacities of rural communities, but of the sociostructural barriers such as poverty, sexism, illiteracy, lack of education, & inadequate employment opportunities for rural women. Rural service providers have devised a host of strategies to implement services, challenge woman-blaming community norms, & resist inappropriate social policy assumptions. The paper concludes that, in combination with the findings of interviews with abused rural women from our earlier research, the perspectives & insights of rural service providers will contribute significantly to a better understanding of the process of implementing effective social policies. 15 References. Adapted from the source document.

Dancing with Diverse Diversities: Creativity and Social Work Professing in Small/Rural Northern Communities

Author: Transken, Si11 Social Work Program, U Northern British Columbia, Prince George si@unbc.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61536274?accountid=14667

Abstract: This is an autoethnographic writing about the importance of creativity in regards to rural social work professing. This article is intended to affirm the complexities that rural social workers may be facing, & it is intended as a guide to those who are considering becoming rural social work educators. As a social work professor teaching & travelling through small communities, I bring with me (un/intentionally) the ghosts & complications of colonialism, the divides between academia & community activism, & the best & the worst of the social work profession's history/herstory. In my work, I am a manifestation of a space between many intersecting hopes, oppressions, diverse needs, flexible realities, & imperfect theories & practices. In the centre of this space I creatively dance & do the best I can. Nelson (2001) & others (Tudiver, 1999) have documented patterns in global shifts in the delivery & meaning of higher education. What impact does this have for the teaching of social work when we are "packaging" & delivering our knowledge "products" to small communities? In small communities there are also issues around overlapping relationships & permeable boundaries. These challenges make the teaching context more stressful, & thus it is all the more important that we map out ways to keep ourselves & our students healthy. There are multiple ways of knowing (First Nations world view, feminist, rural perspectives, etc.) & multiple ways of healing. Creativity, I propose, is a main ingredient in the context of these small communities where I am called to teach & assist in healing processes. Creativity is a natural & renewable resource. Creativity impels us to remain transdisciplinary in our gaze, flexible in our alliances, conscious of our role as organic intellectuals, thoughtful about the multiple oppressions that exist, & curious about how arts & all dimensions of life (not just the clumsy Marxist materialist realities) serve to liberate or arrest individual, organizational, & community development. 41 References. Adapted from the source document.

Home Sweet Home: Experiences of Place for Retirement Migrants to Small-Town British Columbia

Author: MacKenzie, Patricia1; Cloutier-Fisher, Denise1 Faculty Human & Social Development, U Victoria, British Columbia

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61564740?accountid=14667

Abstract: Little is known about retirement migration in Canada, the experiences of retirement migrants & the abilities of small rural communities to support incoming aging residents. This research examines the experiences of retirement in migrants living in the region of "Oceanside," British Columbia. This region incorporates the towns of Qualicum Beach & Parksville. Data from the 2001 Census shows that with a median age of 58.1, Qualicum Beach is the oldest town of all Canadian communities with a population greater than 5,000. Parksville is ranked the fifth oldest in the country with a median age of 49.6. While these communities have grown rapidly in the last decade, an examination of why people choose to relocate to these communities & their experience with relocation is not well documented. This research develops a case study to understand the lived experiences of retirement migrants from an insider view (i.e., in-depth personal interviews) & outsider view (i.e., with local community representatives such as municipal clerks, planners, service providers & realtors). This study will describe how important concepts of in-migration from the Place Identify Model such as identity & sense of community belonging are reflected in the personal narratives of the interviewed respondents. An identification of how these in-migrants adjust to life in their new communities is developed along with the implications of these perspectives on current & near future, service delivery issues. This study is funded by the Social Science & Humanities Research Council of Canada. 31 References. Adapted from the source document.

http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Home+Sweet+Home%3A+Experiences+of+Place+for+Retirement+Migrants+to+Small-Town+British+Columbia&amp;rft.au=MacKenzie%2C+Patricia%3BCloutier-Fisher%2C+Denise&amp;rft.aulast=MacKenzie&amp;rft.aufirst=Patricia&amp;rft.date=2004-12-01&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=129&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Title: Home Sweet Home: Experiences of Place for Retirement Migrants to Small-Town British Columbia

Correspondence author: MacKenzie, Patricia

Living North of 65 Years: A Community-Based Process to Hear the Voices of Northern Seniors

Author: Hemingway, Dawn1; MacLeod, Tom1 U Northern British Columbia, Prince George

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61519915?accountid=14667

Abstract: In an effort to hear the health concerns & needs of senior citizens, the Northern Interior Regional Health Board initiated a community-based participatory process amongst older adults from eight communities across northern British Columbia, Canada. Rather than relying exclusively on the services of researchers at the Health Unit or the University to initiate this consultative process, a more community-controlled route involving eight regionally based Community Advisory Committees (GAGS) & a Task Force on Seniors' Health (each composed of seniors & trusted allies) was adopted. The CACs were the mainstay of the nearly two-year long collaborative process & approved each aspect of the work which included: hosting a local community forum; drafting, revising, piloting & distributing a survey instrument (entitled "Living North of Sixty-Five Years"); organizing a regional seniors' consultative forum; analyzing survey & forum data; identifying the key findings; &, ultimately, presenting recommendations to the Health Board. Successes & limitations of this community-based process are examined along with its potential implications for policy development, service delivery & social work practice with older adults in smaller northern & rural communities. 20 References. Adapted from the source document.

Offering the Help That's Needed: Responses to the Mistreatment and Neglect of Older People in a Rural Canadian Context

Author: Harbison, Joan1; Coughlan, Stephen; Karabanow, Jeff; VanderPlaat, Madine1 Maritime School Social Work, Dalhousie U, Halifax, Nova Scotia joan.harbison@dal.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61538699?accountid=14667

Abstract: The authors discuss the emerging findings of an ongoing study which explores locally occurring, indigenous responses to the mistreatment, neglect & self-neglect of older people in rural communities of an Eastern Canadian province. These responses take place within the context of provincial "adult protection" legislation intended to address "abuse & neglect." The findings so far suggest that professionals & lay people engaged in service delivery are well informed about the limitations of the legislation, understanding that it mandates responses in only a small number of situations. In their own efforts to offer assistance, both professionals & lay helpers are especially concerned about respecting older people's rights & wishes. They also demonstrate caring, creativity & collaboration with others in their efforts to assist. The authors conclude that in order to meet older people's needs, as opposed to simply managing risk, & to prevent their social exclusion, it is important to provide both resources & support for collaborative & innovative ways of helping. 42 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Offering+the+Help+That%27s+Needed%3A+Responses+to+the+Mistreatment+and+Neglect+of+Older+People+in+a+Rural+Canadian+Context&amp;rft.au=Harbison%2C+Joan%3BCoughlan%2C+Stephen%3BKarabanow%2C+Jeff%3BVanderPlaat%2C+Madine&amp;rft.aulast=Harbison&amp;rft.aufirst=Joan&amp;rft.date=2004-12-01&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=147&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Gerontology Education in Rural Areas: Preliminary Results of a Major U.S. Social Work Education Initiative

Author: Dezendorf, Paul1; Lyman, Sue; Green, Ron; Lyman, Scott1 Winthrop U, Rock Hill, SC paul@dezendorf.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61564732?accountid=14667

Abstract: The article describes preliminary results from 37 social work programs serving rural populations regarding their participation in the "GeroRich" initiative, one part of the largest single effort to increase the gerontological content of U.S. social work education. GeroRich was funded by the John A. Hartford Foundation & administered by the Council on Social Work Education (CSWE) & the University of Washington School of Social Work. GeroRich selected 67 programs as a representative sample (11.2%) of total U.S. programs; it included 1,678 faculty & approximately 18,770 students. The funding supported one planning year & two implementation years. Findings from the 37 rural programs of the 67 identified training individual faculty members as key to increasing gerontological content infusion & lack of gerontology content in field experiences as a major weakness. GeroRich appears a useful source of educational innovations & materials for rural social work programs in other countries. 2 Tables, 25 References. Adapted from the source document.

If I Knew Then What I Know Now...A Narrative Perspective on Rural Ministry

Author: Randall, William11 Dept Gerontology, St. Thomas U, Fredericton, New Brunswick randall@stu.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61529041?accountid=14667

Abstract: In this paper, the author draws on his experience as a Protestant minister in rural Canada to outline certain concepts that rural human service workers might do well to keep in mind when attempting to bring a narrative perspective to bear upon their practice. Although he applies this perspective to the dynamics & dilemmas involved when working in the context of organized religion, he argues that a unique type of narrative complexity is integral to rural life in general. Moreover, an appreciation for such complexity is essential, he suggests, for effective rural helping of any kind, not just in ministry but in other professions as well. 50 References. Adapted from the source document.

Creational Capabilities of Language (Language Implications for Our Narratives -- Reflections for Rural Human Service Workers)

Author: Fatemi, Sayyed Mohsen11 U British Columbia, Vancouver sayyedmohsen.fatemi@ubc.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61520573?accountid=14667

Abstract: While examining the creative & creational capacities of language, this paper discusses the significant role of language in our narratives. The paper opens up the room for manifestation of language creativity in our lives & displays how awareness towards the very creativity may allow us to go beyond the prescribed ways of expressiveness. In line with its emphasis on the power of language in questioning the positivist grand narratives (such as grand narratives that assume we all inhabit identical urban rather than diverse rural places), the paper focuses on the vital significance of language education in offering discourses that help people to critically & creatively deconstruct & reconstruct their narratives. 18 References. Adapted from the source document.

Research Notes on Disciplinary Stories by Rural Helpers

Author: Clews, Rosemary1; Randall, William; Furlong, Dolores1 St. Thomas U, Fredericton, New Brunswick clews@stthomasu.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61514047?accountid=14667

Abstract: Shortly before the major data collection phase of a qualitative narrative study, members of the research team gathered with twenty other people who had experiences of helping in rural settings in different countries & different professions. The researchers facilitated discussions about the nature of rural helping & how to prepare students to engage in it. This paper provides their reflections on the process. As such, it can be seen both as a chapter in the narrative about a rural research study & a snapshot of some of the issues about rural helping considered important by the researchers & the participants in the session. 17 References. Adapted from the source document.

Contextual Fluidity: An Emerging Practice Model for Helping

Author: Nelson, Connie1; McPherson, Dennis1 School Social Work, Lakehead U, Thunder Bay, Ontario cnelson@tbaytel.net

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61564703?accountid=14667

Abstract: Societal policies, developed in urban centres under the guise of equality, are often perceived to be in conflict with the well-being of rural areas. Under these policies, emphasis on professional training & appropriate certificates & degrees define the boundaries of who can provide help. These problems have long been acknowledged. Present practice theories perpetuate a classical, modernity approach to the helping process based on the positivist perspective. Codes of ethics insinuate social workers are indeed professionals & therefore are required to be objective with their clients. There is a lack of a viable alternative practice model. This paper recognizes these major reasons for the inability of social work practitioners to move out of this conundrum. A viable alternative practice model, based on principles readily found within any environment, is presented. Contextual Fluidity offers a practice model that overcomes many of the limitations of current social work theories & models. The practice principles of Contextual Fluidity form the critical basis for helping in a manner that is both reciprocal & respectful. 60 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Contextual+Fluidity%3A+An+Emerging+Practice+Model+for+Helping&amp;rft.au=Nelson%2C+Connie%3BMcPherson%2C+Dennis&amp;rft.aulast=Nelson&amp;rft.aufirst=Connie&amp;rft.date=2004-12-01&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=199&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Anti-Racist/Anti-Oppressive Social Work Practice in Northern Rural Communities: Challenges and Considerations

Author: Sangha, David11 Social Work Program, U Northern British Columbia, Peace River-Laird Region, Fort St. John sangha@unbc.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61564434?accountid=14667

Abstract: Much of the current discourse in the social work profession regarding its relationship with First Nations people & racial minorities has shifted from increasing the understanding of the culturally & racially 'other' towards a focus on anti-oppression & antiracism. This focus is evident in its literature, its professional conferences & in its codes of ethics. While this shift is welcome, it nonetheless poses many challenges for social workers working in all settings. This paper will focus on the unique challenges faced by rural social workers in incorporating an anti-racist, anti-oppressive stance in their day-to-day practice. Through an examination of the dynamics of rural practice & the racial hegemony of a small northern rural community, this paper will consider the challenges involved in developing a community-based practice while at the same time addressing the racial inequalities that often pervade such communities. 30 References. Adapted from the source document.

Dealing with Non-Sexual Professional-Client Dual/Multiple Relationships in Rural Communities

Author: Gripton, James1; Valentich, Mary1 Faculty Social Work, U Calgary, Alberta gripton@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61498349?accountid=14667

Abstract: A dual/multiple relationship occurs when there is one or more other relationships beyond the primary relationship of human service provider & client. Establishing & maintaining appropriate boundaries in their relationships with clients can be especially difficult for professionals in small communities & rural areas. Family or friendship ties, lack of alternative professional services, the greater likelihood of chance encounters, involvement in community development activities, & lack of understanding on the part of many clients of boundaries in relationships with service providers -- these can lead to dual/multiple relationships with clients & complicate practice in such locales. This article examines the dynamics of dual/multiple relationships & presents a model for professionals' decision-making & actions when dealing with such situations that commonly arise in rural practice. 27 References. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.atitle=Dealing+with+Non-Sexual+Professional-Client+Dual%2FMultiple+Relationships+in+Rural+Communities&amp;rft.au=Gripton%2C+James%3BValentich%2C+Mary&amp;rft.aulast=Gripton&amp;rft.aufirst=James&amp;rft.date=2004-12-01&amp;rft.volume=9&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=216&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work&amp;rft.issn=13216627&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Classroom Exercises to Enhance Understanding about Diversity in Two Rural Canadian Provinces

Author: Powers, Ann-Marie1; Clews, Rosemary1 Dept Sociology, Acadia U, Wolfville, Nova Scotia apowers@eastlink.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61564403?accountid=14667

Abstract: In this paper a social work educator & an anthropologist explain how they attempt to provide environments where their rural students can learn about diversity. They have designed exercises that are rooted in a shared philosophy of experiential & transformative education. The authors show how the four exercises described in this paper can enable students to experience all four stages of Kolb's experiential learning cycle: concrete experience, reflective observation, abstract conceptualization & active experimentation. Although these exercises have been developed for classrooms in rural Atlantic Canada, the authors propose that they can be adapted for teaching social science & social work students in rural communities in other parts of the world. 29 References. Adapted from the source document.

Geography and Social Worker Retention

Author: Schmidt, Glen1; Klein, Ross1 U Northern British Columbia, Prince George schmidt@unbc.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61528989?accountid=14667

Abstract: Geographic factors exert an influence on social work practice. This is especially true of remote, northern Canadian communities where retention of social workers may be more challenging & difficult because of geography. This paper examines the issue of social worker retention in the field of child welfare. A SWOT (Strengths, Weaknesses, Opportunities, Threats) analysis was used to compare the responses from focus groups comprised of urban & northern child welfare workers. Interviews with supervisors in the urban & northern child welfare practice settings provided further data for comparison. Distinct differences emerged between the urban & northern groups of social workers & supervisors. These differences are examined & discussed in terms of their implications for social worker retention & social work education. 2 Tables, 28 References. Adapted from the source document.

Practitioners Speak -- Balanced Account of Rural Practice, Recruitment and Retention

Author: Lonne, Bob1; Cheers, Brian1 School Social Work & Applied Human Sciences, U Queensland, Brisbane b.lonne@uq.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61520538?accountid=14667

Abstract: A feature of much of the literature & research on rural & remote practice is the tendency to problematise it through commentary & language that focus on the difficulties, challenges & issues it presents, thereby contributing to its generally negative perception as a career option. This paper seeks to provide a more balanced assessment by thematically analysing the narrative accounts from surveys of 194 Australian rural social workers. Respondents celebrated the advantages & acknowledged the disadvantages of rural practice, & also identified ways to address staffing issues. Their important insights into the rewards, demands & challenges of living & practising in rural & remote locations provide opportunities to reconstruct its sometimes-negative professional image, better prepare practitioners to thrive in its prospects, & for employers to use more effective recruitment & retention strategies. 14 References. Adapted from the source document.

Difference and Discrimination in Rural Areas

Author: Pugh, Richard11 Keele U, Staffordshire, UK pughrg@aol.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/61564656?accountid=14667

Abstract: Although the ways in which health, welfare & social services understand difference & discrimination in rural areas may have a significant bearing on how they attempt to meet the needs of minorities, this is an under-theorised & under-researched area of practice. Idealisations of rurality & selective representations may obscure the presence of minorities & lead to a neglect of their needs. However, existing research tends to focus upon the negative experience of minorities & this narrow focus can lead to overly deterministic assumptions about their lives. Acceptance in rural communities is not a simple & static dichotomy of 'in' or 'out', but is a more complex & fluid phenomenon. Differentiation in rural communities can rarely be understood solely in terms of local dynamics, nor simply viewed as mirroring the actions of the wider society. There is a complex interaction between these two dimensions which can produce some unexpected outcomes. The paper concludes by identifying nine factors that may bear upon the local response to individuals from minorities. 22 References. Adapted from the source document.

Building Bridges: A Systematic and Collaborative Approach to Extending Protection and Abuse Prevention Strategies for Women with Intellectual Disabilities in Rural Communities

Author: Macklin, Marie11 School of Community Health, Charles Sturt University in Albury. PO Box 789 ALBURY NSW 2640. Tel: (02) 6051 6736. Fax: (02) 6051 6989 mmacklin@csu.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610901?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article outlines an action research project undertaken in a regional community in Australia aimed at addressing issues of sexuality for people with intellectual disabilities in a regional community. The paper focuses on issues of sexual vulnerability, protection, response to abuse and abuse prevention in a community context. The paper is divided into three main sections. The first section outlines the rationale for the project and its genesis. It then identifies the principles that underpinned the project and briefly discusses the theoretical orientations that informed the project. The second section describes the action research process undertaken in executing the project, focusing specifically on systemic, collaborative and developmental elements of the research strategy. The third section provides some evaluative reflection on some of the more difficult and problematic elements of the project. The paper demonstrates that projects aimed at addressing issues of sexual vulnerability and abuse of people with intellectual disabilities that are informed by community action research have the capacity to promote positive social change. Adapted from the source document.
