My Story/Their Story of Cancer Survival: Reflection from Inside the Rural Research Field Work Experience

Author: Pascal, Jan11 La Trobe University, Bendigo Campus j.pascal@latrobe.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610567?accountid=14667

Abstract: In this article I share my reflections upon the experience of my doctoral research exploring cancer survival within a rural community. I provide an overview of the context for the research that is multifaceted, including demography, literature, personal experience and phenomenological theoretical concepts. The key focus of the article is an exploration of the inter subjective and reflexive role of the researcher, drawing upon my own fieldwork experience. I seek to demonstrate that my 'insider' experience, as cancer survivor and rural resident, challenged and enhanced both my theoretical and personal understanding of this significant life experience. Adapted from the source document.

Rural Families' Response to Chronic Technological Disasters: The Case of the Pigeon River

Author: Soliman, Hussein H11 School of Social Work, Southern Illinois University, Carbondale, IL 62901-4329 soliman@siu.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610605?accountid=14667

Abstract: This case study investigates variations in rural families' responses to chronic technological disasters. As part of a comprehensive field research, the study examines factors that contribute to differences between activist and non-activist families in their response to environmental disasters, and the impacts of social activism on family functioning. Data were collected through interviews, non-participant observation and the review of existing records. While both activist and non activist share similar familial characteristics, activist families seem to adopt positive coping strategies that help reduce the impact of environmental Stressors. Adapted from the source document.

Transparency Status and Institutional Performances of NGOs in Bangladesh

Author: Ullah, AKM Ahsan11 City University of Hong Kong, 83 Tat Chee Avenue, Kowloon Tong, Hong Kong, Tel: 852 2788 9795, Fax: 852 2784 4743 ahsan722000@yahoo.com

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611268?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper aims at exploring the roles of the NGOs capacity building of the rural poor by providing institutional credit and the transparency status of NGOs in Bangladesh. Data were collected through face-to-face interviewing of 96 randomly selected respondents from the beneficiaries of two NGOs in Bangladesh by using both structured and semi-structured questionnaire. Data suggests that all the respondents benefited from NGO institutional credit programmes when judged against the indicators of institutional performances. About one-fourth of the respondents used local arbitration [or shalish] to resolve local problems, while a slightly higher percentage demanded government resources, around 39% took actions against human rights abuses in the society, while about 11% protested dowry demands. About 15% resolved divorce attempts and about 9% received legal aid from the NGOs. Most NGO members were found to use institutional sources for borrowing money. As regards to the transparency and accountability status of the two NGOs, they were found to have very weak transparency. In order to make the NGO programmes effective, they should strengthen the programmes relating to institution building and maintain upwards and downward transparency. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Transparency+Status+and+Institutional+Performances+of+NGOs+in+Bangladesh&amp;rft.au=Ullah%2C+AKM+Ahsan&amp;rft.aulast=Ullah&amp;rft.aufirst=AKM&amp;rft.date=2005-12-01&amp;rft.volume=10&amp;rft.issue=1&amp;rft.spage=32&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Conceptualizing Development: A Comparison of Pakistan Official Plans to Perceptions of Pakistani Rural Poor

Author: Hassan, Bushra1; Stamm, Beth Hudnall1 Institute of Rural Health Studies, Idaho State University, Pocatello, ID 83209-8174

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612011?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper reports on a qualitative study of poverty and development planning in Pakistan, asking if the concept of development varied between rural impoverished people (RP) and the official five-year plans (5P). The methodology used focus groups among 54 people in impoverished rural communities and policy analyses of the plans. The data showed a variety of differences in how the two perceive development. RP perceived development negatively, while 5P considered it positively; RP viewed development experientially, while 5P focused on facts and figures, ignoring psychosocial variables. Together, these factors may explain why those in impoverished rural societies feel dependent, powerless, and isolated, creating social and political alienation from urban areas and the government. The two groups can be integrated through empowerment, decentralisation of development plans, understanding of community importance and values, and enlivening facts and figures with stories to create an experiential embodiment that bridges gaps in society. Adapted from the source document.

Kin Networks in the South: A Comparison of Low-Income, Rural African-American and White Women

Author: Farber, Naomi1; Miller-Cribbs, Julie E; Reitmeier, Melissa C1 University of South Carolina, College of Social Work, Columbia, South Carolina 29208 FAX: 803-777-3498

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610286?accountid=14667

Abstract: In the context of American welfare reform, families in impoverished rural communities increasingly must compensate for reduced public resources while facing less opportunity for employment that offers a living wage. Limited knowledge exists regarding how well kin networks are able to provide resources to poor southern families led by single women. This is a serious gap in critical knowledge given the high rates of single motherhood and poverty among poor families in this and other rural American states. The research reported here examines poor single women's kin networks as a form of social and material support in the context of southern rural poverty in the United States. This study expands knowledge about kinship network structure and function in two important respects: first, by examining kinship networks in an under-studied locale, the American rural south; second, by providing a cross- racial comparison of kinship networks among poor, rural single women. Adapted from the source document.

Homelessness in high income mining towns and the opportunity for big business to play a part

Author: Scheltens, Melinda; Morris, Yolande

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612735?accountid=14667

Abstract: The availability of affordable housing in some rural towns has reached crisis for many families. For some rural towns the landscape is characterised by a lack of housing choices and expensive rent. Enterprises like mines, which are the dominant community industry, have had a marked affect on basic needs like housing. Rents at $300-$400 a week in mining towns makes it untenable for anyone other than high income mine workers. This paper examines the interplay between affordable housing and families, and the implications when income levels change, when couples separate, and when residents are not employed within the mining industry. We highlight experiences of individuals and families living in "high income towns", their housing stress and their experiences and risks of homelessness. This paper explores the opportunity for large industries, like mining corporations, to contribute to the issue of affordable housing in rural areas, and the potential for partnerships between Corporate Australia, Government, Non-Government Organisations and the community to find local solutions to address the problem. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Homelessness+in+high+income+mining+towns+and+the+opportunity+for+big+business+to+play+a+part&amp;rft.au=Scheltens%2C+Melinda%3BMorris%2C+Yolande&amp;rft.aulast=Scheltens&amp;rft.aufirst=Melinda&amp;rft.date=2006-07-01&amp;rft.volume=10&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=6&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Waiting for my job to come back: Rural underemployment in seasonal economies

Author: Gringeri, Christina11 University of Utah, Tel: (801) 581 6191 or fax: (801) 585 3219 cgringeri@socwk.utah.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610551?accountid=14667

Abstract: Seasonal tourist and recreational economies raise questions about economic growth and community development, particularly in rural places. The process of development, as well as the definition of development, are important to the outcomes, especially as experienced by local residents and workers. Most development focuses on economic outcomes while ignoring the social, political and non-material aspects of development for individuals, families and communities. This qualitative study explores development from the perspectives and experiences of local residents and workers, and discusses important ways in which social workers can facilitate a more inclusive process of development in local communities. Adapted from the source document.

Barriers to higher education for Australian rural students

Author: Sewell, Jessica E11 Rural Adolescent Mental Health Group, Centre for Health Research and Practice, University of Ballarat, PO Box 663, Ballarat 3353, Australia. Tel: + 613 5327 9624. Fax: + 61 3 5327 9840 jsewell@students.ballarat.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611762?accountid=14667

Abstract: This article considers the barriers to higher education currently faced by rural students in Australia. Three barriers to participation in higher education for rural students that appear consistently in the literature are financial pressures, family and community attributes, and physical location. Results of recent studies in this area suggest that these factors often coexist to create one, complex and intertwined barrier which needs to be approach by all involved in a collaborative and cooperative way in order to provide rural people with equitable access to higher education. Adapted from the source document.

Country life: Joys, challenges and attitudes of rural social workers

Author: Sidell, Nancy1; Boughton, Beverly; Hull, Patrick; Ertz, Rachel; Seeley, Kayla; Wieder, Jessica1 Mansfield University, 211 Pinecrest, Mansfield, Pennsylvania 16933 570/662-4489 (office); 570-662-4113 (fax nsidell@mansfield.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610593?accountid=14667

Abstract: This paper researched the joys and challenges faced by rural social workers, along with their attitudes about themselves. Licensed social workers residing in one of 14 rural Pennsylvania counties responded to a mailed survey. The sample (n = 117) responded to qualitative questions, including a version of the Twenty Statements Test (TST) that asked "who am I?" Findings indicated that rural practitioners describe themselves as possessing strong familial ties to the communities in which they work. Most expressed enjoyment of a rural lifestyle, and held generally positive attitudes about themselves. Implications for future practice are addressed. Adapted from the source document.

The emergence of water markets in Australia and implications for rural social work

Author: Mason, Robyn1; McDonald, John; Ollerenshaw, Alison1 Centre for Health Research and Practice, University of Ballarat, PO Box 663, Ballarat Vic Australia 3353. Tel: (03) 5327 9629. Fax: (03) 5327 9538

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612889?accountid=14667

Abstract: The aim of this paper is to consider the implications for rural social work practice of the widespread and severe drought coupled with the emergence of water markets in Australia. The National Water Initiative was signed at the June 2004 Council of Australian Governments meeting with the aim of producing a nationally-compatible, market, regulatory and planning-based system of managing water resources to optimise economic, social and environmental outcomes. The National Water Commission and the National Competition Council have since assessed progress on the implementation of the initiative: none of their reports gives adequate consideration to the impact of water reform on rural communities. In this paper, we draw upon previous research and written submissions made to the Commission and the Council to examine the social and political consequences of the drought and the emergence of water markets. We discuss the implications for rural practice, and conclude by proposing seven recommendations to assert the role of rural social workers as change agents. This role could encompass community education and advocacy, piloting schemes such as community water banks, reinstating community development in social work curriculum, and facilitating collaborative rural partnerships. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=The+emergence+of+water+markets+in+Australia+and+implications+for+rural+social+work&amp;rft.au=Mason%2C+Robyn%3BMcDonald%2C+John%3BOllerenshaw%2C+Alison&amp;rft.aulast=Mason&amp;rft.aufirst=Robyn&amp;rft.date=2006-12-01&amp;rft.volume=11&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=6&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Working with retrenched timber workers and impacted communities: Use of a community development approach

Author: Laing, Gail1; Hepburn, Mike1 798 Hazelvale Rd, Hazelvale WA Australia 6333. Tel: (08) 9840 8421 gaillaing@yahoo.com.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610298?accountid=14667

Abstract: In 2001 the Western Australian government altered its policy with regard to logging in old growth south western forests. The reduction to logging quotas impacted significantly on those employed in the timber industry and the supporting infrastructure. This paper describes a community development project aimed to assist local residents come to terms with this significant change to livelihood and income potential. The Project focused on meeting and managing the challenge of the social transition from a well understood and accepted timber industry and associated community culture towards a much reduced level of logging and alternative employment activities. Adapted from the source document.

Mental health reform: An informal rural network implements mental health reform for parent consumers

Author: Zeman, Laura Dreuth1; Buila, Sarah1 School of Social Work Southern Illinois University, Mail Code 4329 Carbondale, Illinois USA 62901. Tel: (618)457-2224

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612054?accountid=14667

Abstract: This study sought to understand how a rural network responded to mental health reform and to suggest program direction for further implementation of best practice standards. Five focus groups consisting of 36 participants included professionals employed in schools, community mental health, family court, and as disability advocates in rural southern Illinois, United States. Strengths, weakness, opportunities, threats (SWOT) analyses identified the network's current functioning. Threats, opportunities, weaknesses, strengths (TOWS) analyses generated insight on how to further implement best practice standards. The highest priorities were the infrastructure linkages, care models where setbacks are a part of recovery, consumer support systems, respecting consumers, and consumer-directed care. Implications include creating more flexible programs, expanding peer support, and integrating evidenced based practice into programs. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Mental+health+reform%3A+An+informal+rural+network+implements+mental+health+reform+for+parent+consumers&amp;rft.au=Zeman%2C+Laura+Dreuth%3BBuila%2C+Sarah&amp;rft.aulast=Zeman&amp;rft.aufirst=Laura&amp;rft.date=2006-12-01&amp;rft.volume=11&amp;rft.issue=&amp;rft.spage=30&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Providing social care services in rural Australia: A review

Author: Mason, Robyn11 School of Humanities & Social Sciences, Charles Sturt University, Locked Bag 678, Wagga Wagga NSW Australia 2678. Tel: (02) 6933 2478. Fax: (02) 6933 2792 rbmason@csu.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611232?accountid=14667

Abstract: The features of Australia's rural context have been explored by social researchers over the past two decades, and we now have a growing body of knowledge about how rural Australians live. There is also an emerging literature about rural social care practice. As a service provider, my interest is in how services are planned and implemented, in the light of the Australian rural context. This paper reviews the relevant literature in the emerging field of Australian rural social care service provision, organised under five main headings or research threads. These are: urbo-centric issues; generalist and specialist practice issues; challenges in recruitment, retention and support; boundary problems of living in small communities; and access to services. Analysis of the research in this way suggests that there is not yet a strong theoretical base in this field, with the literature dominated by practice accounts and case studies. A solid base for rural social research, in partnership with rural communities, is required in Australian universities, especially those located in rural Australia, if the gaps are to be filled and the promise of this emerging field is to be realized. Adapted from the source document.

Interpretive phenomenological approaches to rural mental health research

Author: Aisbett, Damon L11 Rural Adolescent Mental Health Group, Centre for Health Research and Practice, University of Ballarat, PO Box 663, Ballarat Vic Australia 3353. Tel: + 61 3 5327 9624. Fax: +613 5327 9840 c.boyd@ballarat.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611971?accountid=14667

Abstract: While researchers in the fields of social work and nursing have recognised the usefulness of qualitative research methods for many years, researchers in psychology have tended to lag behind in their understanding of the value and applicability of qualitative approaches. The aims of this article are to consider the role of qualitative research in psychology, to outline the various steps involved in conducting qualitative data collection and Interpretive Phenomenological Analysis (IPA) for those not versed in these techniques, and to provide a case example of the applicability of these methods to rural adolescent mental health research. The final section of this article includes a detailed discussion of the advantages and limitations of IPA. Adapted from the source document.

____________________________________________________________

Rural practitioners of the future: Views of graduating students about rural child and family practice

Author: McAuliffe, Donna1; Chenoweth, Lesley; Stehlik, Daniela1 School of Human Services, Griffith University, University Drive, Meadowbrook QLD 4131 Australia. Tel: +61 7 3382 1070. Fax: +61 33821210 d.mcauliffe@griffith.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611488?accountid=14667

Abstract: The difficulty in attracting practitioners to rural and remote areas is well documented. However little empirical evidence is available about the perceptions that potential social work and human services recruits have of rural practice in Australia, and what factors might improve recruitment and retention. This paper reports on findings of the first stage of a larger study which explored both the views and experiences of final year social work and human services students, and existing child and family practitioners working in rural communities. The first phase of the study aimed to identify student experiences and views of living and working in rural communities, the perceived barriers that could prevent them from applying for positions in rural and remote areas, and incentives/strategies they thought might attract workers to rural and remote locations. Key findings were that work with children and families are popular post-graduation preferences, and despite lack of knowledge and ambivalence about rural practice, the majority were prepared to consider rural opportunities in the interests of career advancement and perceived lifestyle benefits. Students provided a range of suggested strategies that employing organizations could adopt to attract applicants, and improve rates of retention in rural child and family practice. These findings have international application as the literature on recruitment and retention reveals many similarities across different countries and among different professional disciplines. Adapted from the source document.

Toward responsive community services: Consumer participation in a rural counselling service

Author: Eversole, Robyn1; Brumby, Susan; Jack, Evelyn1 Institute for Regional Development, University of Tasmania

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611717?accountid=14667

Abstract: Designing services to meet communities' needs requires understanding clearly what those needs are. Ideas about grassroots, community-based, decentralised or participatory development all promote active involvement of communities in developing services. More recently, 'consumer participation' has become a hot topic. Yet questions persist about the mechanics of encouraging participation, and how to ensure that it is both authentic and inclusive. This paper reflects on a case from rural Australia in which a community-based health provider has piloted a process for encouraging local participation in the ongoing development of its counseling service. The focus was on current, past, and potential users of this service ('consumers') from rural towns and properties of the region. The project developed a mechanism for consumers to provide comment and input, toward the goal of making the service as responsive as possible to community needs. This paper describes the participation process and reflects upon the outcomes and what was learned about the costs, benefits, and usefulness of implementing such a process. Adapted from the source document.

Restructuring the governance of child welfare in a remote, resource based community: Implications of economic crisis and community change

Author: Gillespie, Judy Lillian11 School Of Social Work 3333 University Way Kelowna, BC Canada V1V 1V7 Tel: (250) 807-8745 fax: (250) 807-8792 gillespie@ubc.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612004?accountid=14667

Abstract: It can be assumed that the "good governance" of child welfare is no different in a single industry "instant town" than in any other type of town. In other words, it relies on healthy families, cohesive communities, and a range of adequate services and supports. However, single industry towns, common in the Canadian north, face unique challenges in achieving these. The following paper highlights some of these challenges based on a case study of the town of Grande Cache, Alberta, at a time when it was undergoing economic crisis and significant community change. The impact of these changes on the Regional Child Welfare Authority's efforts to restructure community roles and relations is also noted. It is argued that efforts to build community capacity to address child welfare must take into account the unique dynamics of specific community contexts and find ways to work effectively with and within them. Adapted from the source document.

Welfare workers' experiences of rural practice -- similar but different

Author: Green, Rosemary11 Victoria, Queensland, the Northern Territory

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741609946?accountid=14667

Abstract: This research project investigated the experiences of six rural welfare professionals living in the Western region of Victoria, Australia. In particular, this paper reports on their experiences of managing dual and multiple roles, confidentiality and their privacy. The six participants, five women and one man, were purposely chosen for in-depth interviews using criterion sampling from 23 survey respondents who participated in a larger rural practice study. Issues of particular interest to participants were managing confidential information gathered from formal and informal sources, providing services that are non-stigmatising, managing complex ethical dilemmas arising from dual and multiple roles, and lack of anonymity and privacy. Participants' experiences of these issues had varied considerably. Some expressed feeling of anxiety and stress, while others expressed strong confidence about their management of these matters in their daily practice. Many participants were anxious about whether their practice fully met the ethical guidelines of the profession, however they felt their practice appropriate to the context. Most of the participants commented that their pre-service education did not adequately address rural issues or prepare them for their work role in small communities. Adapted from the source document.

Practicing as a critically reflective rural social worker

Author: Western, Deb

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611839?accountid=14667

Nonsexual dual and multiple relationships: "When urban worldviews define rural reality"

Author: Bodor, Ralph C11 Faculty of Social Work, University of Calgary. Tel: 1-780-686-0432 rcbodor@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612700?accountid=14667

Abstract: Social workers in rural and remote communities have found the occurrence of nonsexual dual and multiple relationships to be a constant practice issue. Most existing ethical codes and standards of practice contain caveats prohibiting or advising against nonsexual dual relationships and, while it is often acknowledged that human service workers in rural and remote communities must experience these types of relationships more frequently than workers in an urban context, there is minimal research that directly addresses this acknowledgment. This Canadian study provides a deeper understanding of the experience of nonsexual dual and multiple relationships for social workers in rural and remote communities. It was decided that the research would approach the issue from the perspective of philosophical hermeneutics; a worldview that grants value to the contexts and understandings of place, history, experiences, voices, and text. Hermeneutics found its roots in a response to the empirical, rationalist thought of the Enlightenment and, as a consequence of employing a hermeneutic perspective, the subsequent loss of subject/object separation allows the experiences of rural and remote social work practice-including that of dual and multiple relationships -to be understood differently. It would appear that the empirical "urban" valuation of meaning has resulted in the disqualification of what is truly understood to be rural and remote-including the experience of being connected to others and to community and suggesting that dual and multiple relationships are a valuable and inseparable component of rural and remote social work practice. Adapted from the source document.

Cashing in on social capital: Subsidizing low-wage work in Utah's rural areas

Author: Gringeri, Christine E11 College of Social Work, University of Utah USA. Tel: 801-581-4864

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612193?accountid=14667

Abstract: Social capital is an important strategy that many rural families in the Intermountain West (USA) use to offset inadequate employment and meet their needs. Based on open-ended, qualitative interviews with sixty families in rural Utah, however, it becomes apparent that their reliance on social capital for survival and progress toward economic stability is more than a way to subsidize low-wage work. The use of social capital subsidizes employers by cushioning workers, and it helps offset retrenchment in public welfare provision by providing many items, such as housing, childcare, food, and cash. Adapted from the source document.

Experience of place for adolescents in rural Australia

Author: Newnham, Karyn1; Boyd, Candice; Newnham, Krystal; Francis, Kristy; Aisbett, Damon1 University of Ballarat, PO Box 663 Ballarat Vic 3353 Australia

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611738?accountid=14667

Abstract: Researchers in the field of rural mental health in Australia now realize the need for a more in-depth understanding of the relationship between people and places to fully appreciate the nature of mental health issues within the rural Australian context (Fraser et al., 2005). The meaning of place and place attachment for rural adolescents is highly significant as characteristics of rural communities have the potential to create a risk to psychological health during the important developmental stage of adolescence (Boyd et al., 2006). The present study aims to explore the experience of place for adolescents in rural Victoria, Australia using a phenomenological approach to qualitative data collection and analysis. This analysis revealed that the rural adolescents who participated in this study had a strong, positive sense of place with themes broadly representing place attachment, belonging to place, and place identity. The adolescents were conscious that they belonged but this was dependant on obeying the expected cultural rules of their place. The role that physical surroundings played in these adolescents' self-concept, identity and life history was expressed in notions of past and present pride in association; expressing place in relation to self-identity; and affirmations that one 'belonged' as a product of establishing genetic history. These adolescents perceived their place to be supportive of general life and physical challenges but not mental health issues. Adapted from the source document.

Closed doors and culture wars: Contemporary challenges for human services delivery in rural and regional Australia

Author: Grant, Julie1; Francis, Karen1 Gippsland Medical School, Monash University, Australia

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610521?accountid=14667

Abstract: Recent qualitative research confirms that rural and regional Australians do not enjoy equitable access to social care services. This paper discusses the social impacts of class and cultural change on rural communities, drawing upon qualitative data generated during a multi-state study of rural social care provision, undertaken in four Australian states during 2005 -- 2006. Participants were drawn from a wide range of professional groups, including social workers; nurses, including Aboriginal health workers; community development and local government officers; teachers, including student welfare coordinators; police; ambulance officers; emergency services workers; and medical practitioners. Findings based on the Victorian data suggest that existing policies are failing to address cultural and institutional barriers to equitable service provision. Further research is needed to identify successful strategies that service providers are utilising to overcome existing cultural barriers and institutional hurdles. Adapted from the source document.

Gaining acceptance in rural communities: A study of young families who relocate to non-metropolitan areas in Australia

Author: Hillman, Wendy11 Dept of Behavioural and Social Sciences, Building 32, CQUniversity, Bruce Highway, Nth Rockhampton, QLD, Australia 4701. Tel: +61 7 4930 9289 Fax: +61 7 4930 6460 w.hillman@cqu.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612717?accountid=14667

Abstract: Text of abstract Recently in Australia there has been a demographic shift in the movements of young families away from capital cities. There is some evidence to suggest that many of the families who do move to regional or rural communities face issues of isolation, lack of social support networks and the added obstacle of not being accepted into their new community. The vulnerability of these families places them in an 'at risk' situation, where they can become isolated and excluded, as newcomers, from their immediate neighbours and townsfolk and they can be treated as if they are not a part of their new community. This paper focuses on how families, with young children aged 0-8 years, gain acceptance in non-metropolitan areas of Australia after relocation. Issues of both social inclusion and social exclusion form the underlying basis of the feelings the newcomers experience upon their arrival in their new location. The paper will discuss the findings from two project research sites and will analyse the challenges to acceptance faced by the young families in this study. Community level strategies that enable the young families to gain acceptance, and general promotion of community acceptance of the newcomers also forms part of the research analysis of these issues. Adapted from the source document.

'With resources we could do anything': Responding to the needs of Australian rural women

Author: Mason, Robyn11 Department of Social Work, Faculty of Medicine, Nursing and Health Sciences, Monash University, Caulfield East, Victoria, Australia. Tel: 03 9903 1136 (BH robyn.mason@med.monash.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612725?accountid=14667

Abstract: Rural women-specific service providers in Australia face similar challenges to those faced by rural services everywhere: access, confidentiality, isolation, lack of resources and an expectation that an urban service model will fit the rural context. They face additional challenges because they confront unsettling and contentious issues, such as violence against women and women's rights, often in hostile environments. This paper reports findings from a national study of 74 rural women-specific service providers who were asked to say how services could be strengthened or enhanced. Participating services suggested seven areas of change where they considered services could be improved: recognition of rural disparity; more resources; better networks and linkages; more support for feminist services; increased community education about women's issues; improved transport options; and better support for workers. Their suggestions reveal that they are strongly connected to the local community context, unafraid of taking a political stand and overtly feminist in their approach. This group of service providers is well-informed and can offer valuable insights for policy makers into how best to meet women's needs in non-metropolitan settings. Adapted from the source document.

Socioeconomic status as a mediator of the relationship between depression and rurality in Australian adolescents

Author: Newnham, Krystal1; Boyd, Candice; Newnham, Karyn; Aisbett, Damon; Francis, Kristy1 Orygen Research Centre, University of Melbourne, Locked Bag 10, Parkville 2052, Australia

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611525?accountid=14667

Abstract: The purpose of this study was to establish whether socioeconomic status would mediate the relationship between rurality and depression in a sample of Australian adolescents. Participants were 65 rural and 64 urban secondary school students from Years 11 and 12 who completed a survey that included a brief demographic questionnaire and the Centre for Epidemiological Studies -- Depression Scale. Schools were selected for their degree of rurality as measured by the Accessibility and Remoteness Index for Australia, and the socioeconomic status of each individual was estimated via postal code using the Socio-Economic Index of Australia. Although a significant relationship between degree of rurality and depression was found, socioeconomic status did not mediate this relationship. These results suggest that the relationship between rurality and depression for young people is not due to simple differences in socioeconomic status between urban and rural areas. Other features of 'place' must be considered in accounting for rural-urban differences in adolescent depression. Adapted from the source document.

Key influences on rural health and welfare service delivery: Lessons from the literature

Author: Gregory, Raeleene1; Green, Rosemary; McLaren, Suzanne1 University of Ballarat, School of Behavioural and Social Science and Humanities. Tel: 03 5327 9619 r.gregory@ballarat.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741612713?accountid=14667

Abstract: Contemporary rural Australia is home to a diverse population which constitutes approximately one third of the total population of the nation. Rural Australians are engaged in numerous occupations and undertakings, not all of which are directly involved with agriculture. A decline in the agricultural sector over past decades has, however, led to significant disadvantage in terms of the resources and facilities available to rural people. There are issues regarding recruitment and retention of staff which provide challenges for health and welfare agencies. The provision of effective services is dependent on staff; however, there are a number of influences on the experience of health and welfare professionals in the Australian rural context. This paper, through a review of the literature, identifies some of those influences. Adapted from the source document.

____________________________________________________________

Confronting a research quandary: Accounting for 'self-excluders' in rural research

Author: Hillman, Wendy1; Healy, Karen; Hampshire, Anne1 Institute of Health and Social Science Research, Department of Behavioural and Social Sciences, CQUniversity, Australia, Bruce Highway, North Rockhampton, Queensland, Australia 4702. Tel: +61 7 4930 9289. Fax: +61 7 4930 6460 w.hillman@cqu.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611476?accountid=14667

Abstract: Researchers have frequently confronted dilemmas regarding rigor and validity in their methodological approaches to social inquiry. Problems with access to participants, and indeed, to particular subject groups who do not wish to be identified, much less become part of a formalised research project, are some of the dilemmas facing many researchers. Yet, these reluctant individuals can provide vital insights into the processes of isolation and social exclusion. It is all very well 'to go to the people' (Bogdan and Taylor 1998), but how can 'the people' be identified and studied, if they do not wish to be recognised, contacted or found? In this paper we focus on a 'self-excluder' as an illustrative case study of a larger research project about young families with small children, aged 0-8 years, who choose to live on the fringes of society. Initial access to these 'self-excluders' also causes dilemmas, as they are hard to find (Vobruba 2000). First, identifying them, as individuals, and second, gaining access to them through appropriate and ethical research techniques and methods are main points for concern and resolution. Many of these challenges are explored in this paper. Adapted from the source document.

Experiences of sexual coercion, awareness of services, and acceptance of rape myths among students in rural colleges

Author: Vanderwoerd, James R11 Redeemer University College, 777 Gamer Road E., Ancaster, Ontario, L9K 1J4. (905) 648-2139 x4434 jwoerd@redeeemer.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611824?accountid=14667

Abstract: College campuses in rural communities are often thought to be safer than urban campuses, but unfortunately, anecdotal evidence suggests that sexual coercion and intimate partner violence also occur at rural colleges. This paper reports on a research study investigating the experiences of students at three rural college campuses -- two of which are faith-based -- in the US Midwest. Overall, the prevalence of sexual coercion and violence was comparable to rates reported in previous studies of urban colleges and universities. Gender of the victim was the most significant variable associated with experiencing sexual coercion or violence. Religious affiliation of the college had no significant relationship to experiences of sexual victimization, and made only small differences in acceptance of rape myths. Adapted from the source document.

Rural communities in America: Two case studies employing total quality management consensus-building to focus groups

Author: Barton, Barbara1; Hagerty, Jennifer; Miller, Dawn1 4462 Hemmingway Kalamazoo, Michigan, United States 49009 Barbara.barton@wmich.edu

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741611195?accountid=14667

Abstract: Small communities face unique place-based challenges for the community organizer. This manuscript is a case study of two small villages in Michigan USA whose leaders participated in Total Quality Management (TQM) structured focus groups, and whose residents were sampled and individually interviewed. Results indicate that community connectedness is hampered by inadequate communication mechanisms between village/township leaders and residents. Residents of both communities were also sensitive to the fact that the exporting of labor hinders opportunities for economic stability and growth. Adapted from the source document.

A future for rural social work in Victoria: Developing a framework for working in uncertainty: Keynote address, 9th Biennial Rural Remote Social Work Conference, Geelong, Victoria, Australia 30-31 July 2009

Author: Mason, Robyn11 Department of Social Work, Monash University, Australia robyn.mason@med.monash.edu.au

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/741610549?accountid=14667

Abstract: The questions I want to address in this paper are: First: In a time of rapid change and much uncertainty, how can we position ourselves to be active agents in the creation of sustainable rural communities? Second: given the social impacts of change -- including climate change -- how can we ensure that our expertise in managing uncertainty is recognized by decision-makers and policy makers and that our voices are heard? The paper has a specific focus on rural Victoria, Australia, but the issues raised are rapidly becoming universal. My intention is to outline a particular view from rural Victoria in 2009; features of an uncertain future; how social work approaches uncertainty; how rural social work is located in this debate; and, finally, some recommendations for action. Adapted from the source document.

Disordered Dependencies: The Impact of Language Loss and Residential Schooling on Indigenous Peoples

Author: Makokis, Leona11 Faculty Social Work, Blue Quills First Nations Coll, U Calgary, Alberta leonam@bluequills.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/753992656?accountid=14667

Abstract: In this paper I utilise my firsthand experience of residential school to illustrate the impact of colonization, specifically loss of language, on the Indigenous peoples of Canada. The highly complex role of language is explored on both cultural & spiritual levels. This examination, in relation to historical context, allows pertinent observations to be drawn regarding the current state of affairs for Canadian Indigenous people. Adapted from the source document.

Bringing Home the Kids

Author: Bodor, Ralph1; Lamourex, Rochelle; Biggs, Holly1 Faculty Social Work, U Calgary, Alberta rcbodor@ucalgary.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/753992758?accountid=14667

Abstract: The destructive processes of colonization & assimilation of First Nations & Metis people in Canada has not come to an end rather, like a chameleon, these forms of oppression simply change their appearance & continue on. From the tragedy of Residential Schools, to the ravages of the Sixties Scoop, to the incremental devastation of family & community by the bureaucratic process of putting children "in-care," the cumulative negative impacts of contact with Western society remain. In 2006, in a northern Alberta (Canada) First Nations community of 8000 people, approximately 150 children were "in-care" & living two hours away in a large urban centre with minimal contact with their home community. Community-based Children's Services (Wahkohtowin) decided it was time to "Bring Home the Kids" -- & staged an event designed to reconnect these children to their community. While it was acknowledged that a single event will not solve the problem of the number of children taken into care, it was felt that this opportunity to reconnect would help with the issues of anxious & disrupted attachment that many of the children (& the community) were experiencing -- & would also help to increase community awareness of the number of children who are in care. Perhaps it would eventually lead to repatriation. It was a place to continue the process of healing. This is the story of that event. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Bringing+Home+the+Kids&amp;rft.au=Bodor%2C+Ralph%3BLamourex%2C+Rochelle%3BBiggs%2C+Holly&amp;rft.aulast=Bodor&amp;rft.aufirst=Ralph&amp;rft.date=2009-12-01&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=12&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Bringing+Home+the+Kids&amp;rft.au=Bodor%2C+Ralph%3BLamourex%2C+Rochelle%3BBiggs%2C+Holly&amp;rft.aulast=Bodor&amp;rft.aufirst=Ralph&amp;rft.date=2009-12-01&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=12&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Social Work Counseling Using the Medicine Wheel

Author: Sundlie, Monique11 Blue Quills First Nations Coll, U Calgary msundlie@telus.net

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/753992890?accountid=14667

Abstract: The Medicine Wheel was historically & is currently being used by Canadian Indigenous & Metis people to conceptualize the seemingly abstract notion of "wellbeing." The personal Medicine Wheel specifically identifies the Physical, Emotional, Mental & Spiritual aspects of humanity. Each person carries within them a metaphorical Medicine Wheel. which the individual may easily refer to by sketching a Medicine Wheel on paper, & accounting for how each aspect is represented in their life. Using the Medicine Wheel technology has enabled Canadian social workers to make intangible concepts & abstract theories about wellness. significantly more concrete for the client. The Medicine Wheel is especially useful for counseling from a strengths-based perspective. Some Canadian social workers have adopted & adapted the Medicine Wheel philosophies in their counseling practice. which is why there are many different applications of Medicine Wheel technology. One application focuses the social work counselor's practice model on addressing Wholeness, Balance, Harmony & Healing within the counselor/client relationship, which in turn, strengthens the client's personal Medicine Wheel. This literature review identifies the helper's practice model of the Medicine Wheel with the meta-themes of Wholeness, Balance, Harmony & Healing throughout the paper. It explores the history, context & usefulness of Medicine Wheel technology in social work counseling, including for use inside & out of the Indigenous context. Adapted from the source document.

Repatriating Canada's Indigenous Children: Planning for the Future

Author: Chewka, Derek11 Faculty Social Work, Blue Quills First Nations Coll, U Calgary, Alberta dchewka@shaw.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/853217036?accountid=14667

Abstract: For the past few years we have heard the term "repatriation" used to refer to the bringing home of Canadians that have died overseas while serving in the Armed Forces. Somewhere along the way, the Government has taken that term & used it to refer to the process of reintegrating children or "bringing them back home" to their community of origin. There is no clear translation in the Cree language for repatriation. The reason is simple -- it is a term that was given to them by the government. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Repatriating+Canada%27s+Indigenous+Children%3A+Planning+for+the+Future&amp;rft.au=Chewka%2C+Derek&amp;rft.aulast=Chewka&amp;rft.aufirst=Derek&amp;rft.date=2009-12-01&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=29&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Addressing the Culture of Expectancy: The Sharing of Self for the Collective Wellness of Community

Author: Makokis, Tracey L11 Faculty Social Work, Blue Quills First Nations Coll, U Calgary, Alberta Tracey.Makokis@gov.ab.ca

http://proxy.lib.umich.edu/login?url=http://search.proquest.com/docview/753992715?accountid=14667

Abstract: The culture of expectancy is a pervasive issue that is detrimental to the overall health & well being of Canadian First Nation communities. A Federal government policy change is required- one that shifts transfer payments from focusing on social welfare, to one that concentrates on economic development. However, this policy must be initiated from the Canadian First Nation people to ensure it is a 'good fit' for the clients who will be impacted, as well as effectively combating notions of dependency & entitlement that have been cultivated by the Colonization process. Community investment policies are one example of how First Nations communities have an opportunity to provide a culturally relevant approach, while also meeting current Canadian demands for labor -- a win-win situation that benefits everyone. A thorough policy analysis, utilizing a number of specific criteria, demonstrates that 'work for welfare' policies are viable & realistic. Further, implementation would have much support; reciprocity is an acknowledged value within the Cree world-view & much of the community infrastructure required for this policy already exists. The proposed legislated expectation is that every individual on social assistance contribute a specific number of hours every month to collective initiatives. This approach honors traditional values, while building human capital -- with enormous potential for proactive community building. Adapted from the source document.

Links: http://mgetit.lib.umich.edu/?ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;ctx_enc=info:ofi/enc:UTF-8&amp;rfr_id=info:sid/ProQ%3Asocialservicesshell&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;rft.genre=article&amp;rft.jtitle=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.atitle=Addressing+the+Culture+of+Expectancy%3A+The+Sharing+of+Self+for+the+Collective+Wellness+of+Community&amp;rft.au=Makokis%2C+Tracey+L&amp;rft.aulast=Makokis&amp;rft.aufirst=Tracey&amp;rft.date=2009-12-01&amp;rft.volume=14&amp;rft.issue=2&amp;rft.spage=38&amp;rft.isbn=&amp;rft.btitle=&amp;rft.title=Rural+Social+Work+and+Community+Practice&amp;rft.issn=18333060&amp;rft_id=info:doi/
